reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Model that performs a transposed 3D convolution, applies ReLU, and then applies group normalization.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, groups, bias=False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, bias=bias)
        self.relu = nn.ReLU()
        self.group_norm = nn.GroupNorm(num_groups=groups, num_channels=out_channels)

    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, D, H, W).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, D, H, W).
        """
        x = self.conv_transpose(x)
        x = self.relu(x)
        x = self.group_norm(x)
        return x

batch_size = 16
in_channels = 64
out_channels = 128
D, H, W = 32, 32, 32
kernel_size = 3
groups = 8
bias = False

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, D, H, W)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, groups, bias]

For ConvTranspose3d, the operation is: output[n, :, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{kd, kh, kw} input[n, c_in, d', h', w'] * weight[c_in, :, kd, kh, kw] where d = d' + kd, h = h' + kh, w = w' + kw (with appropriate stride/upsampling). For stride=1, padding=0: d = d' + kd, so d' = d - kd h = h' + kh, so h' = h - kh w = w' + kw, so w' = w - kw So: output[n, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in=0}^{63} sum_{kd=0}^{2} sum_{kh=0}^{2} sum_{kw=0}^{2} input[n, c_in, d-kd, h-kh, w-kw] * weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] With the constraint that 0 <= d-kd < 32, etc. This is exactly a regular convolution with flipped kernel and expanded output! Actually wait, for ConvTranspose3d with stride=1, padding=0, the output size is input_size + kernel_size - 1 = 34. This matches. We can think of it as: for each input element, we add a weighted kernel to the output. This is the "scatter" view of transposed convolution. Or we can think of it as: for each output element, we compute a dot product with a window of the input. This is the "gather" view. For a direct CUDA kernel, the gather view is more natural: For each output position (n, c_out, d, h, w), compute the dot product over the input window. The dimensions are: - Batch: 16 - Out channels: 128 - Out D: 34 - Out H: 34 - Out W: 34 We could tile across these dimensions. The innermost loop runs over in_channels * 27 = 1728 elements. For a thread, doing 1728 multiply-adds is reasonable. But we need to be careful with memory access patterns. That's a stride of 3456 * 4 bytes = 13.8KB. Actually, for weight accesses, if a warp of 32 threads all computes different (n, d, h, w) but same c_out, they all read weight[c_in, c_out, ...] for the same (c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw). So all 32 threads read the exact same weight value - perfect for broadcast/L1 cache. For input access: input[n, c_in, d', h', w'] where d' = d - kd, etc. With different (n, d, h, w) in the warp, the accesses to input are scattered across different spatial locations. But if we structure so that threads in a warp handle consecutive W positions, then for fixed (n, c_in, d', h'), threads access input[n, c_in, d', h', w'], w', w'+1, w'+2, etc. which are consecutive in memory (assuming NCHW or NCDHW layout where W is contiguous). Actually, PyTorch uses NCDHW: N, C, D, H, W. So W is the contiguous dimension. If we tile by W, then adjacent threads in a warp process adjacent W positions. For the same (n, c_in, kd, kh, kw), input[n, c_in, d-kd, h-kh, w-kw] for w = w0, w0+1, ... w0+31 would access consecutive memory. But only if d-kd and h-kh are the same. If different threads are at different d, h positions, then even with adjacent w, the memory access isn't contiguous. So better structure: each thread block handles a tile of (D, H, W) for a specific (batch, channel out), and threads within a block are assigned consecutive W positions for a specific (D, H). Actually, a common strategy for 3D conv is: - Each thread computes one or more output elements - We can load input tiles into shared memory to reuse data Given that kernel_size=3 and we have plenty of shared memory, we could load input tiles and compute from shared memory. ConvTranspose3d with kernel_size=3, stride=1 is actually equivalent to a 3D convolution with padding=2 (if we use the transposed weight). Specifically: ConvTranspose3d(in, out, 3, stride=1, padding=0, bias=False) is the same as: Conv3d(in, out, 3, stride=1, padding=2, bias=False) but with weight permuted from (in, out, 3, 3, 3) to (out, in, 3, 3, 3) AND with the spatial dimensions of the weight reversed (i.e., weight[:, :, kd, kh, kw] -> weight[:, :, 2-kd, 2-kh, 2-kw]). Wait, is that correct? Let me verify. Standard Conv3d: output[n, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} input[n, c_in, d + kd - pad, h + kh - pad, w + kw - pad] * weight[c_out, c_in, kd, kh, kw] With padding=2: output[n, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} input[n, c_in, d + kd - 2, h + kh - 2, w + kw - 2] * weight[c_out, c_in, kd, kh, kw] For this to equal ConvTranspose3d: ConvTranspose3d output: output[n, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} input[n, c_in, d - kd, h - kh, w - kw] * weight_t[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] where 0 <= d - kd < 32, etc. Let me substitute in the Conv3d formula. Let d' = d + kd - 2. For d=0, d' = kd - 2. If kd=0, d'=-2 (invalid). kd=1, d'=-1 (invalid). kd=2, d'=0. So for d=0, only kd=2 contributes, and input[n, c_in, 0, ...] * weight[c_out, c_in, 2, ...]. But in ConvTranspose3d, for d=0, only kd=0 contributes: input[n, c_in, 0, ...] * weight_t[c_in, c_out, 0, ...]. So we need weight[c_out, c_in, 2, ...] = weight_t[c_in, c_out, 0, ...]. More generally, for the formulas to match, we need: weight[c_out, c_in, kd, kh, kw] used at spatial offset (d + kd - 2, ...) should match weight_t[c_in, c_out, kdt, kht, kwt] used at offset (d - kdt, ...). So d + kd - 2 = d - kdt => kdt = 2 - kd. And weight[c_out, c_in, kd, kh, kw] = weight_t[c_in, c_out, 2-kd, 2-kh, 2-kw]. So yes! ConvTranspose3d(in_channels=64, out_channels=128, kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=0) is exactly equivalent to Conv3d(in_channels=64, out_channels=128, kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=2) with the weight transposed and spatially flipped. Actually, the weight shape for Conv3d is (out_channels, in_channels, kD, kH, kW) = (128, 64, 3, 3, 3). And if we take the ConvTranspose3d weight of shape (64, 128, 3, 3, 3), transpose dims 0 and 1 to get (128, 64, 3, 3, 3), then flip spatially in dims 2,3,4. Tiles starting at 0 and 28, 32 are boundary (since 32+4>34). Actually tile start positions: 0, 4, 8, 12, 16, 20, 24, 28, 32 (but 32+4=36>34, so we'd only process 32..33). Tiles starting at 0, 28, 32 are boundary, and tiles starting at 4, 8, ..., 24 are interior. That's 6 interior out of 9 = 66%. Even boundary tiles are partially interior. If we use larger tiles (e.g., 8 or 16), the fraction of interior tiles increases. So boundary handling with if-statements is probably fine. But actually, for simplicity and to avoid any divergence, we can pad the input with explicit zero padding and use the padded input pointer. Inside the kernel, we'd just read from the padded input without boundary checks. The padding can be done on-the-fly in shared memory: when loading an input tile, the halo regions (near boundaries) would be zeros. But this requires knowing the tile position relative to the boundary. Yes, this is the standard approach: when loading shared memory, threads check if the source position is within the original input bounds, and write 0 otherwise. For interior tiles, all positions are valid. For boundary tiles, some halo positions are zeroed. After loading, the compute loop has no boundary checks. This is cleaner! And the loading overhead is small since boundary tiles are few. On modern GPUs, a broadcast from shared memory is very fast - all threads get the value in a single cycle. This is actually ideal! Wait, that's even better than no bank conflicts. It's a broadcast! So as long as all threads in the warp access the same shared memory address, it takes just one cycle (or at least it's as fast as any shared memory access). So the compute loop is: for ic = 0 to 7: for kd = 0 to 2: for kh = 0 to 2: for kw = 0 to 2: w = weight_smem[0..3][ic][kd][kh][kw] (broadcast for threads, sequential in registers for the 4 channels) in_val = input_smem[ic][d_thread+kd][h_thread+kh][w_thread+kw] acc[0..3] += w[0..3] * in_val The inner loop runs 8 * 27 = 216 iterations, each doing 4 FMAs per thread. Total FMAs per thread: 864. Plus the remaining loop for channels (8 slices). Wait, each thread computes 4 output channels. For each (ic, kd, kh, kw), the thread does 4 FMAs (one per oc). For GroupNorm, we need to compute mean and variance per (batch, group). Since each block only computes 4 output channels, which belong to a single group (since groups=8, channels_per_group=16, and 4 channels is 1/4 of a group). So we need to accumulate statistics across blocks that compute the same group. We can have each block write its partial sum and sum-of-squares to a global reduction buffer. Then a second kernel reduces and computes inv_std. A third kernel normalizes. Alternatively, we can have the first kernel write the ReLU output to a temporary buffer, and then a single group norm kernel reads it, computes the stats per group (using shared memory reductions), and writes normalized output. This is simpler. PyTorch's group norm reads all elements twice (once for mean, once for variance, or once for both using Welford). A custom kernel can do a single-pass read using Welford's algorithm in shared memory. Since each (N, group) has 16 * 39304 = 628,864 elements, and we have 16 * 8 = 128 such groups. For the group norm kernel: - Each block handles one (N, group). - Threads in the block process the 628,864 elements. - First, compute mean using parallel reduction. - Then compute variance using parallel reduction. - Then normalize and write output. Wait, for exact numerical equivalence with PyTorch's group norm, we need to use the same algorithm. PyTorch typically uses a two-pass or Welford approach. Let me use a two-pass for simplicity: 1. Compute sum in parallel (blockReduce) 2. Compute mean = sum / count 3. Compute sum of squared differences from mean in parallel 4. Compute variance = sum_sq_diff / count 5. Compute inv_std = rsqrt(variance + eps) 6. Normalize: (x - mean) * inv_std * weight[c] + bias[c] But steps 1-5 and 6 can be fused into a single kernel that reads x once, accumulates in shared memory, synchronizes, then reads x again? Or we can read x once, keep it in registers if possible? No, with 628k elements and 256 threads, each thread would need 2456 elements in registers - way too many. So a two-read approach: - First phase: all threads collaboratively compute sum and sum_sq (or just sum, then mean, then second pass for variance). - Second phase: all threads read x again and write normalized output. Actually, we can do a two-pass within the same kernel: Pass 1: load chunk of elements, accumulate local sum and local sum_sq. Then block reduce to get mean and variance. Pass 2: reload same chunk, normalize, write output. This reads the input twice but avoids a temporary buffer for the intermediate output. Wait, we're reading the conv output. If the conv output is already in a temporary buffer, then reading it twice is fine. So the full pipeline: 1. Kernel 1: conv + ReLU, write to temp buffer. 2. Kernel 2: group norm (two-pass), read from temp, write final output. Kernel 1 writes ~322MB. Kernel 2 reads ~322MB twice and writes ~322MB. Total memory: ~1.3GB. At 3.35TB/s, this is ~0.4ms. The conv dominates with ~8-15ms. But we can try to do better: if we accumulate partial statistics during the conv kernel and write them to a small global buffer, we might save the temp buffer read for group norm. But the reduction across blocks still requires synchronization. Wait, if we write partial statistics from each conv block, the stats buffer size is small: 16 batches * 8 groups * 2 (sum and sum_sq) * 4 bytes = ~1KB. Then a reduction kernel computes final stats, and a normalization kernel reads the conv output and normalizes. But we still need to read the conv output for normalization. Alternatively, if we accumulate stats in the conv kernel but don't write the conv output until after normalization... but we can't normalize until we know the stats, and stats require all blocks to finish. So we definitely need either: a) Temporary buffer for conv output, or b) Run conv twice (once for stats, once for normalized output) Option (a) is clearly better. The temporary is 322MB, and reading it for normalization adds ~644MB of memory traffic (read twice for two-pass, or read once for Welford). Total extra memory: 322MB write + 644MB read = 966MB. At 3.35TB/s = 0.29ms. Negligible compared to conv time. So let's go with: 1. Fused conv + ReLU kernel, writes to workspace. 2. GroupNorm kernel, reads workspace, computes stats, normalizes, writes to final output. Now, can we make the conv kernel faster by using the NHWC (channels last) layout? Actually, in the compute loop, for a given ic_local, all threads in the block read some spatial position for that ic_local. The innermost loop should be over kw (contiguous in input) for better cache locality. But in our case, input is in shared memory, so all accesses are the same speed. Still, keeping kw innermost is conventional. For the weight, if it's in global memory and we don't use shared memory for weights: weight[oc_local][kd][kh][kw][ic_local] with ic_local varying innermost would be contiguous. Loading from shared memory vs L1 cache might not make much difference. But if all threads in the block read the same weight, L1 cache can broadcast. If threads read different weights for different oc, then L1 might not handle it as well. Actually, if a warp reads weight for 4 different oc values, and the weight is in global memory with layout (OC, kD, kH, kW, IC), then threads accessing different oc are far apart in memory (stride = 3*3*3*64 = 1728 floats = 6912 bytes). That's a scatter access and not coalesced. But if all threads in a warp access the SAME oc at the same time (e.g., they all loop over oc sequentially), then the access is uniform broadcast. Good. In our design, each thread handles 4 output channels sequentially. So at any moment, a thread accesses one oc value. Different threads might be at different spatial positions but the SAME oc. If they have different oc_thread, they read different weight addresses. With weight in shared memory, this is fine (different banks if the layout avoids conflicts, which we checked). With weight in global memory, this would be scattered access. So shared memory for weights is beneficial. OK, I think I have a solid design. Let me also consider using constant memory for weights. Weights are only 128 * 64 * 27 * 4 = ~884KB. That's larger than the 64KB constant memory limit. But we could cache the active weight tile in shared memory. Actually, 884KB is small enough to fit in L2 cache (50MB on H100). So global memory accesses to weights will mostly hit L2. But shared memory is still faster. For our weight tile of 3.5KB, loading it from global to shared memory once per block is very cheap. Let me now think about the group norm kernel. GroupNorm kernel design: - Input: (N, C, D, H, W) = (16, 128, 34, 34, 34) after conv+ReLU - Output: same shape - C=128, groups=8, so C_per_group = 16 - For each (n, g) where g is group index 0..7: - Compute mean over c in [g*16, g*16+15], and all spatial positions 34^3 - Compute variance similarly - Normalize: (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * gamma[c] + beta[c] The count per group is 16 * 39304 = 628,864 elements. We can assign one block per (n, g). That's 128 blocks. With 128 SMs on H100, we can run one block per SM, but we want more parallelism. Alternatively, multiple blocks per (n, g), and use global atomics for the reduction. But 128 blocks is too few to saturate the GPU. Better: each block handles one (n, g) and multiple blocks handle different (n, g). Still only 128 blocks. Or each block handles a subset of the spatial positions for one (n, g), then reduce. With 128 blocks and 144 SMs, we have decent utilization if each block does enough work. Let's see: each block processes 628,864 elements. With 256 threads, each thread processes ~2456 elements. That's a lot of work. The reduction in shared memory is doable. Wait, 2456 elements per thread. Reading them in a loop: - Each thread maintains local sum and local sum_sq (Welford) for its elements. - After all elements are processed, do warp shuffle + shared memory reduction. - Then compute mean and inv_std. - Then each thread re-reads its elements and normalizes them. But with 2456 elements, and needing to read them twice, that's ~10k float reads per thread. In global memory, coalesced reads. But each block only has 2456 * 256 = 628k elements, which is ~2.5MB. Reading twice is 5MB per block. 128 blocks = 640MB total reads + 320MB writes = ~1GB memory traffic. This should take ~0.3ms. The loop structure: for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < elements_per_group; idx += blockDim.x) { // Map idx to (c_local, d, h, w) } Since elements_per_group = 16 * 39304 = 628864, and blockDim.x = 256, each thread handles 2456 iterations. Mapping idx: c_local = idx / 39304, spatial = idx % 39304. spatial -> d = spatial / (34*34), h = (spatial / 34) % 34, w = spatial % 34. In global memory, elements for consecutive idx have consecutive c_local only after every 39304. So consecutive threads access different spatial positions but the SAME c_local for the first 39304 threads. Since C_per_group = 16, threads 0-255 with idx = 0..255 all access c_local=0, and spatial=0..255, which are mostly contiguous in W for fixed d,h. Actually, for c_local fixed at 0: idx = spatial = d*34*34 + h*34 + w. Consecutive idx: w increments, then h, then d. So consecutive threads access consecutive W, which is contiguous in memory! Coalesced! When idx wraps around to c_local=1, the address jumps by 39304 floats = 157KB. Still coalesged within the warp since all threads jump together. So global memory access is highly coalesced. For the reduction: Each thread computes local_sum and local_sum_sq (or M2 for Welford). Then do blockReduce using shared memory. For 256 threads, reduction takes log2(256) = 8 steps. Negligible. Then each thread normalizes its elements and writes back. Coalesced writes. For the gamma and beta: they are per-channel, shape (128,). For c_local in [0,15] within the group, actual channel = group*16 + c_local. The normalization formula in PyTorch GroupNorm with affine=True: y = (x - mean) * rsqrt(var + eps) * weight[c] + bias[c] We need to match PyTorch's computation exactly. PyTorch uses Welford or a two-pass algorithm. Let me check what numerical equivalence requires. PyTorch GroupNorm computation (simplified): mean = x.mean(dim=[2,3,4,...], keepdim=True) # over channels-in-group and spatial var = x.var(dim=[2,3,4,...], unbiased=False, keepdim=True) # population variance x = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) if affine: x = x * weight + bias Float32. We need our output to match within 1e-4 absolute or relative. For numerical stability, PyTorch uses Welford for variance computation, but the result is very close to the naive formula: var = E[x^2] - E[x]^2. The naive formula suffers from catastrophic cancellation. Welford is more stable. Since our data is ReLU output (positive, mean around some positive value), the naive formula might be OK, but Welford is safer. However, implementing Welford in CUDA with parallel reduction is a bit more complex but doable. Actually, a simpler approach that matches PyTorch well: 1. Use atomicAdd to global memory to compute exact per-(N, group) sum and sum-of-squares. Since there are only 128 groups and atomics are fast (especially on H100), this might be efficient. 2. Then normalize in a second kernel or the same kernel after a grid sync (not possible). Wait, for atomics to work, all blocks computing the same group need to write to the same global accumulator. Since each (N, group) is handled by potentially many blocks in the conv kernel... but in our group norm kernel, each block handles a subset of one (N, group). So we can use block-level reduction to shared memory, then one thread per block writes to global atomic. But that's only 1 atomic per block, not per element. Very fast! Actually, even better: we can compute the mean and variance across all elements of the group using a multi-block reduction. Algorithm for group norm with multiple blocks per group: - If we have 128 blocks total and 128 (N, group) pairs, we can just use 1 block per group. No need for inter-block reduction. Kernel 2c: group_norm_apply - each block handles one channel, reads conv output, normalizes using stats, writes final output. Wait, that's 3 kernels for group norm plus 1 for conv = 4 kernels total. For 2a: 2048 blocks, each reading 39304 floats = ~157KB. Total reads: 2048 * 157KB = 320MB. This is the conv output. Writes: 2048 * 2 * 4 = 16KB. Very small. For 2b: 128 blocks, reading 16 partial sums each = 128 * 16 * 2 * 4 = 16KB. Negligible. For 2c: 2048 blocks, reading 39304 floats and writing 39304 floats per block. Total: 320MB read + 320MB write. For now, let's not overthink this and write the kernel. Here's the CUDA kernel plan: ```cuda template<int BLOCK_OC, int BLOCK_OW, int BLOCK_OH, int BLOCK_OD, int IC_BLOCK> __global__ void conv3d_relu_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int IC, int ID, int IH, int IW, int OC, int OD, int OH, int OW, int pad_d, int pad_h, int pad_w ) { // Block indices int batch = blockIdx.z; int oc_block = blockIdx.y * BLOCK_OC; int spatial_block = blockIdx.x; // Decompose spatial_block into (d_tile, h_tile, w_tile) int num_w_tiles = (OW + BLOCK_OW - 1) / BLOCK_OW; int num_h_tiles = (OH + BLOCK_OH - 1) / BLOCK_OH; int d_tile = spatial_block / (num_h_tiles * num_w_tiles); int rem = spatial_block % (num_h_tiles * num_w_tiles); int h_tile = rem / num_w_tiles; int w_tile = rem % num_w_tiles; int d0 = d_tile * BLOCK_OD; int h0 = h_tile * BLOCK_OH; int w0 = w_tile * BLOCK_OW; // Thread indices int tid = threadIdx.x; int w_out = tid % BLOCK_OW; int h_out = (tid / BLOCK_OW) % BLOCK_OH; int d_out = tid / (BLOCK_OW * BLOCK_OH); int d = d0 + d_out; int h = h0 + h_out; int w = w0 + w_out; bool valid = (d < OD && h < OH && w < OW); // Shared memory // Weights: [BLOCK_OC][IC_BLOCK][3][3][3] // Input tile: [IC_BLOCK][BLOCK_OD+2][BLOCK_OH+2][BLOCK_OW+2] extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_weight = smem; float* smem_input = smem + BLOCK_OC * IC_BLOCK * 27; int ID_pad = ID + 2 * pad_d; int IH_pad = IH + 2 * pad_h; int IW_pad = IW + 2 * pad_w; // Accumulators float acc[BLOCK_OC]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < BLOCK_OC; i++) acc[i] = 0.0f; // Iterate over input channels in blocks for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < IC; ic_base += IC_BLOCK) { // Load weights into shared memory // Weight layout: (OC, kD, kH, kW, IC) int num_weights = BLOCK_OC * IC_BLOCK * 27; for (int widx = tid; widx < num_weights; widx += blockDim.x) { int kw = widx % 3; int wh = (widx / 3) % 3; int wd = (widx / 9) % 3; int ic_local = (widx / 27) % IC_BLOCK; int oc_local = widx / (27 * IC_BLOCK); int oc = oc_block + oc_local; int ic = ic_base + ic_local; if (oc < OC && ic < IC) { int weight_idx = ((oc * 3 + wd) * 3 + wh) * 3 + kw; weight_idx = weight_idx * IC + ic; smem_weight[widx] = weight[weight_idx]; } else { smem_weight[widx] = 0.0f; } } // Load input tile into shared memory int IN_D = BLOCK_OD + 2; int IN_H = BLOCK_OH + 2; int IN_W = BLOCK_OW + 2; int in_tile_size = IN_D * IN_H * IN_W; for (int iidx = tid; iidx < IC_BLOCK * in_tile_size; iidx += blockDim.x) { int ic_local = iidx / in_tile_size; int sp = iidx % in_tile_size; int iw = sp % IN_W; int ih = (sp / IN_W) % IN_H; int id = sp / (IN_W * IN_H); int ic = ic_base + ic_local; int id_in = d0 + id; int ih_in = h0 + ih; int iw_in = w0 + iw; if (ic < IC && id_in < ID_pad && ih_in < IH_pad && iw_in < IW_pad) { int in_idx = ((batch * IC + ic) * ID_pad + id_in) * IH_pad * IW_pad + ih_in * IW_pad + iw_in; smem_input[iidx] = input[in_idx]; } else { smem_input[iidx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); if (valid) { // Compute #pragma unroll for (int ic_local = 0; ic_local < IC_BLOCK; ic_local++) { #pragma unroll for (int kd = 0; kd < 3; kd++) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int id_sm = d_out + kd; int ih_sm = h_out + kh; int iw_sm = w_out + kw; float in_val = smem_input[(ic_local * in_tile_size + id_sm * IN_H * IN_W + ih_sm * IN_W + iw_sm)]; #pragma unroll for (int oc_local = 0; oc_local < BLOCK_OC; oc_local++) { float w_val = smem_weight[(oc_local * IC_BLOCK + ic_local) * 27 + kd * 9 + kh * 3 + kw]; acc[oc_local] += in_val * w_val; } } } } } } __syncthreads(); } // ReLU and write output if (valid) { #pragma unroll for (int oc_local = 0; oc_local < BLOCK_OC; oc_local++) { int oc = oc_block + oc_local; if (oc < OC) { float val = acc[oc_local]; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); int out_idx = ((batch * OC + oc) * OD + d) * OH * OW + h * OW + w; output[out_idx] = val; } } } } ``` Wait, I need to be more careful with the shared memory indexing for input. Then we combine pairs of (mean, M2, count) using the parallel Welford algorithm. Finally, variance = M2 / count. Let me implement Welford for kernel 2a: ```cuda struct WelfordData { float mean; float m2; int count; }; __device__ WelfordData welford_combine(WelfordData a, WelfordData b) { if (a.count == 0) return b; if (b.count == 0) return a; int count = a.count + b.count; float delta = b.mean - a.mean; float mean = a.mean + delta * b.count / count; float m2 = a.m2 + b.m2 + delta * delta * a.count * b.count / count; return {mean, m2, count}; } ``` In the kernel: ```cuda WelfordData local = {0, 0, 0}; for (int idx = start; idx < end; idx += stride) { float x = input[idx]; local.count++; float delta = x - local.mean; local.mean += delta / local.count; float delta2 = x - local.mean; local.m2 += delta * delta2; } // warp reduce // block reduce // write to global ``` For kernel 2b, read the 4 partial results per group, combine them, and compute inv_std = rsqrtf(local.m2 / local.count + eps). Now let me think about numerical equivalence with PyTorch. PyTorch Conv3d uses whatever CuDNN algorithm it chooses. Our custom kernel uses the direct formula. Wait, actually, I'll use Welford in our kernel! In the conv kernel, instead of accumulating sum and sum_sq, we can accumulate using Welford's online algorithm. But Welford needs elements processed in sequence, and then we need to combine across threads using the parallel Welford algorithm. The parallel Welford combining is: Given (n1, mean1, M21) and (n2, mean2, M22): n = n1 + n2 delta = mean2 - mean1 mean = mean1 + delta * n2 / n M2 = M21 + M22 + delta * delta * n1 * n2 / n This is exact and numerically stable. In each thread, we accumulate local Welford state over its elements. Then we combine within the warp, then within the block. Then thread 0 writes the block's (count, mean, M2) to global memory. After all blocks finish, a second kernel combines the per-block Welford states for each group and computes inv_std = rsqrtf(M2 / count + eps). For the conv kernel, the local Welford accumulation per thread: ```cpp float mean = 0.0f; float m2 = 0.0f; int count = 0; // for each valid output element val after ReLU: count++; float delta = val - mean; mean += delta / count; float delta2 = val - mean; m2 += delta * delta2; ``` Since each thread computes 4 output channels and some spatial positions, it loops over all its valid elements. Wait, but the thread computes 4 output values inside the conv loop (one per oc_local). It can't do Welford on the fly because the values are produced inside the compute loop. After the compute loop, it has 4 values. Then it can do Welford over those 4 values. But there might be fewer than 4 valid values if oc >= OC or spatial position invalid. After the compute loop, the thread does: ```cpp float mean = 0, m2 = 0; int count = 0; if (valid) { for (int oc_local = 0; oc_local < BLOCK_OC && oc_block + oc_local < OC; oc_local++) { float val = acc[oc_local]; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); output[...] = val; count++; float delta = val - mean; mean += delta / count; float delta2 = val - mean; m2 += delta * delta2; } } ``` Then warp reduce, then block reduce. But wait, Welford combine needs to be done carefully. Let me write device functions: ```cpp struct Welford { float mean; float m2; int count; }; __device__ Welford welford_init() { return {0.0f, 0.0f, 0}; } __device__ void welford_step(Welford& state, float x) { state.count++; float delta = x - state.mean; state.mean += delta / state.count; float delta2 = x - state.mean; state.m2 += delta * delta2; } __device__ Welford welford_merge(Welford a, Welford b) { if (a.count == 0) return b; if (b.count == 0) return a; int count = a.count + b.count; float delta = b.mean - a.mean; float mean = a.mean + delta * b.count / count; float m2 = a.m2 + b.m2 + delta * delta * a.count * b.count / count; return {mean, m2, count}; } __device__ Welford warp_reduce_welford(Welford val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { Welford other; other.mean = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.mean, offset); other.m2 = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.m2, offset); other.count = __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val.count, offset); val = welford_merge(val, other); } return val; } ``` In the block reduction, after warp reduce, we need to reduce across warps using shared memory. There are 256/32 = 8 warps. ```cpp __shared__ Welford s_welford[8]; Welford local = ...; // computed per thread // Warp reduce local = warp_reduce_welford(local); if (threadIdx.x % 32 == 0) { s_welford[threadIdx.x / 32] = local; } __syncthreads(); // Block reduce if (threadIdx.x < 8) { local = s_welford[threadIdx.x]; local = warp_reduce_welford(local); // only need reduce over 8 elements, but warp_reduce does 32. As long as the other 24 are zeros. if (threadIdx.x == 0) { // write to global atomics or directly int group = oc_block / channels_per_group; atomicAdd(&global_mean_n[batch * num_groups + group], local.mean * local.count); atomicAdd(&global_m2[batch * num_groups + group], local.m2); atomicAdd(&global_count[batch * num_groups + group], local.count); } } ``` Wait, this uses atomicAdd on mean*count (which is sum) and m2. But atomicAdd on floats is not associative in the same way. Actually, for the mean, we want to accumulate sum and M2 separately, then compute mean = sum / total_count. And var = M2 / total_count. So we can just atomicAdd `sum`, `m2`, and `count` (with integer atomicAdd for count). For atomicAdd on `sum` and `m2`, we need to be careful. PyTorch computes variance as population variance (divides by count, not count-1). If we accumulate `sum` and `sum_sq` (E[x^2] style), we can compute var = sum_sq/count - (sum/count)^2. But this has precision issues. If we accumulate M2 using Welford, then var = M2 / count. This is better. But atomicAdd on M2 from Welford: each block's M2 is valid for its subset. Can we just add M2 values from different blocks? For Welford, the M2 values from disjoint subsets CAN be combined using the merge formula, but they can't just be added. M2 total != M2_a + M2_b. So we either: 1. Use the merge formula in the reduction kernel (not in atomics). 2. Use a two-pass approach: accumulate sum with atomicAdd (which is exact for count, and sum is associative enough). Then compute mean = total_sum / total_count. Then a second pass computes sum of squared deviations. But this requires a second read of the data. Method 1 is better for a single pass. The subtraction 0.26 - 0.25 = 0.01. With FP32 having ~7 decimal digits, and both terms being ~0.26 and ~0.25 computed from sums of 628k elements, the relative error might be small. Actually, the sum of 628k elements of ~0.5 gives ~314k. In the apply kernel: ```cpp float mean = group_mean[n * num_groups + g]; float inv_std = group_inv_std[n * num_groups + g]; float gamma_c = gamma[c]; float beta_c = beta[c]; float normalized = (x - mean) * inv_std * gamma_c + beta_c; ``` This matches PyTorch exactly. One optimization: we can store mean and inv_std in shared memory or registers if they're reused. In the apply kernel, all threads in a block for the same channel read the same mean and inv_std. Let me write the apply kernel: ```cuda __global__ void group_norm_apply_kernel( float* __restrict__ output, const float* __restrict__ group_mean, const float* __restrict__ group_inv_std, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, int N, int C, int D, int H, int W, int num_groups, int channels_per_group ) { int n = blockIdx.y; int c = blockIdx.x; int g = c / channels_per_group; float mean = group_mean[n * num_groups + g]; float inv_std = group_inv_std[n * num_groups + g]; float g_val = gamma[c]; float b_val = beta[c]; int spatial_size = D * H * W; int base_idx = ((n * C + c) * D) * H * W; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < spatial_size; idx += blockDim.x) { float x = output[base_idx + idx]; float y = (x - mean) * inv_std * g_val + b_val; output[base_idx + idx] = y; } } ``` Wait, I'm reading and writing the same buffer `output`. Is this OK if threads don't race? Since each thread writes the exact index it reads, and no other thread accesses that index, it's fine. Now for the stats compute kernel: ```cuda __global__ void compute_stats_kernel( const float* __restrict__ group_sum, const float* __restrict__ group_sum_sq, float* __restrict__ group_mean, float* __restrict__ group_inv_std, int N, int num_groups, int count, float eps ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * num_groups; if (idx >= total) return; float sum = group_sum[idx]; float sum_sq = group_sum_sq[idx]; float mean = sum / count; float var = sum_sq / count - mean * mean; if (var < 0) var = 0; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); group_mean[idx] = mean; group_inv_std[idx] = inv_std; } ``` This is very simple. Let me write a block reduction for sum: ```cuda __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); return val; } __inline__ __device__ float block_reduce_sum(float val) { __shared__ float sdata[8]; // one per warp int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; val = warp_reduce_sum(val); if (lane_id == 0) sdata[warp_id] = val; __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { val = (lane_id < 8) ? sdata[lane_id] : 0; val = warp_reduce_sum(val); } return val; } ``` Since there are 8 warps in a block of 256 threads, this works. Now for the conv3d_relu kernel with atomics, I'll make sure to initialize the group_sum and group_sum_sq tensors to 0 using `torch::zeros`. Actually, within the CUDA kernel, atomicAdd on float requires `#include <cuda_atomic.h>` or just works with `<cuda_runtime.h>`. In C++ extensions, it should just work. One concern: floating-point atomicAdd performance. On H100, atomicAdd for float is fast, especially when contention is low. With 16 blocks per group and each doing one atomicAdd, contention is minimal. Let me verify: blocks per group = (OC / BLOCK_OC) * spatial_tiles = (128/4) * 225 = 32 * 225 = 7200 blocks per batch. Wait, no! For a fixed (batch, group): Channels in group = 16. Channel blocks = 16 / 4 = 4. Spatial tiles = 225. Blocks per group = 4 * 225 = 900. And each of these 900 blocks does atomicAdd to the same two float addresses (sum and sum_sq for that group). Is 900 atomicAdds to the same address low contention? That's actually quite a lot. But in practice, the GPU has many atomic units, and with a 16-batch problem, there are 128 different atomic addresses, so the load is distributed. Still, 900 atomics to the same address might have some serialization. Alternative: use a separate scratchpad for block-level partial sums. Each block writes its sum to a unique address. Then reduce in a second kernel. This avoids all contention! Scratchpad size: total blocks = N * (OC/BLOCK_OC) * spatial_tiles = 16 * 32 * 225 = 115,200 blocks. Each block writes sum and sum_sq. So scratchpad = 115,200 * 2 floats = 230,400 floats = ~921KB. That's fine. Then in the reduce kernel, for each group: - Identify all blocks that belong to the group and sum their values. This is simple if the blocks are ordered in a specific way. Block index structure: blockIdx = (spatial_tile, oc_block, batch) For a fixed group g, the oc_blocks are g*4 and g*4+1, g*4+2, g*4+3? Wait, BLOCK_OC=4, channels_per_group=16, so oc_block values for group g are: g*16, g*16+4, g*16+8, g*16+12. With spatial_tiles = 225, there are 4 * 225 = 900 blocks per group. If I write the partial sums in the natural block order, the reduce kernel needs to know which blocks belong to which group. Mapping block index to linear block ID: linear_id = ((batch * (OC/BLOCK_OC) + oc_block_idx) * spatial_tiles + spatial_tile) Wait, blockIdx.y = oc_block_idx (0..31), blockIdx.x = spatial_tile (0..224), blockIdx.z = batch (0..15). linear = blockIdx.z * 32 * 225 + blockIdx.y * 225 + blockIdx.x. For group g, valid oc_block_idx: g*4, g*4+1, g*4+2, g*4+3. Corresponding linear IDs: batch * 7200 + g * 4 * 225 + [0, 1, 2, 3] * 225 + spatial_tile. So in the reduce kernel, for group (batch, g): partial sums are at indices: base + offset, where base = batch * 7200 + g * 900, and offset ranges 0..899. Wait, linear_id for oc_block=g*4 and spatial=0: batch*7200 + g*4*225 + 0 linear_id for oc_block=g*4 and spatial=224: batch*7200 + g*4*225 + 224 linear_id for oc_block=g*4+1 and spatial=0: batch*7200 + g*4*225 + 225 So the 900 consecutive partial sums for group (batch, g) range from: start = batch * 7200 + g * 900 end = start + 899 Because (g*4)*225 = g*900. And the next 4 oc blocks cover exactly 900 consecutive linear IDs. This is beautiful! So the scratchpad can be indexed linearly by block ID. In the conv kernel, we write: ```cpp int block_linear = blockIdx.z * gridDim.y * gridDim.x + blockIdx.y * gridDim.x + blockIdx.x; partial_sums[block_linear] = block_sum; partial_sum_sq[block_linear] = block_sum_sq; ``` No atomics needed! Then in the reduce kernel: ```cpp __global__ void reduce_stats_kernel( const float* partial_sums, const float* partial_sum_sq, float* group_mean, float* group_inv_std, int N, int num_groups, int blocks_per_group, int count, float eps ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * num_groups; if (idx >= total) return; int batch = idx / num_groups; int g = idx % num_groups; int base = batch * (num_groups * blocks_per_group) + g * blocks_per_group; // Wait, blocks per group = (channels_per_group / BLOCK_OC) * spatial_tiles = 4 * 225 = 900. float sum = 0; float sum_sq = 0; for (int i = 0; i < blocks_per_group; i++) { sum += partial_sums[base + i]; sum_sq += partial_sum_sq[base + i]; } float mean = sum / count; float var = sum_sq / count - mean * mean; var = fmaxf(var, 0.0f); float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); group_mean[idx] = mean; group_inv_std[idx] = inv_std; } ``` This reduction reads 900 floats sequentially per thread, which is slow if done by one thread. Better to use multiple threads per group or a parallel reduction. Actually, with 128 groups and 900 partial sums each, total partial sums = 115,200. We can launch a reduction kernel with 256 threads per block and enough blocks. But a simpler approach: launch 128 blocks (one per group), each with 256 threads. Each thread sums a subset of the 900 partial sums (about 4 per thread), then block reduce. ```cuda __global__ void reduce_stats_kernel(...) { int batch = blockIdx.y; int g = blockIdx.x; int idx = batch * num_groups + g; float local_sum = 0; float local_sum_sq = 0; int base = idx * blocks_per_group; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < blocks_per_group; i += blockDim.x) { local_sum += partial_sums[base + i]; local_sum_sq += partial_sum_sq[base + i]; } local_sum = block_reduce_sum(local_sum); local_sum_sq = block_reduce_sum(local_sum_sq); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { float mean = local_sum / count; float var = local_sum_sq / count - mean * mean; var = fmaxf(var, 0.0f); group_mean[idx] = mean; group_inv_std[idx] = rsqrtf(var + eps); } } ``` Grid: (num_groups, N, 1) = (8, 16, 1) = 128 blocks. Each block has 256 threads. blocks_per_group = 900. Loop: for (i=threadIdx.x; i<900; i+=256), each thread sums 4 elements. Very fast. This is much better than atomics! And the scratchpad memory is only ~921KB, written once and read once. Let me use this approach. Pipeline: 1. `conv3d_relu_kernel`: computes conv+ReLU, writes to temp_output, writes partial_sums and partial_sum_sq. 2. `reduce_stats_kernel`: reads partial_sums, computes mean and inv_std. 3. `group_norm_apply_kernel`: reads temp_output, normalizes, writes final output to temp_output. All clean, no atomics in the main path! For shared memory usage in the conv kernel, the block sizes are fixed. Let me tune BLOCK_OD, BLOCK_OH, BLOCK_OW, BLOCK_OC, IC_BLOCK for good performance. The current values (4, 8, 8, 4, 8) seem decent. But I might try a few variations if the first attempt isn't fast enough. Actually, let me think about register pressure. Each thread has: - 4 accumulators for output channels - Loop variables - Shared memory pointer offsets - local_sum, local_sum_sq for stats That's about 10-15 float/int registers. Plus the compiler might unroll loops and use more. Should be well under 64 registers. Now for the Python side, I need to load the C++ extension. The complete Python file: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # C++ and CUDA source cpp_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define BLOCK_OC 4 #define BLOCK_OW 8 #define BLOCK_OH 8 #define BLOCK_OD 4 #define IC_BLOCK 8 #define WARP_SIZE 32 // Block reduce sum using warp shuffles __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = WARP_SIZE / 2; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } return val; } __inline__ __device__ float block_reduce_sum(float val) { __shared__ float sdata[8]; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / WARP_SIZE; int lane_id = threadIdx.x % WARP_SIZE; val = warp_reduce_sum(val); if (lane_id == 0) sdata[warp_id] = val; __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { val = (lane_id < 8) ? sdata[lane_id] : 0.0f; val = warp_reduce_sum(val); } return val; } __global__ void conv3d_relu_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, float* __restrict__ partial_sums, float* __restrict__ partial_sum_sq, int N, int IC, int ID, int IH, int IW, int OC, int OD, int OH, int OW, int channels_per_group ) { const int IN_D = BLOCK_OD + 2; const int IN_H = BLOCK_OH + 2; const int IN_W = BLOCK_OW + 2; const int IN_TILE_SIZE = IN_D * IN_H * IN_W; const int NUM_WEIGHTS = BLOCK_OC * IC_BLOCK * 27; int batch = blockIdx.z; int oc_block = blockIdx.y * BLOCK_OC; int spatial_block = blockIdx.x; int num_w_tiles = (OW + BLOCK_OW - 1) / BLOCK_OW; int num_h_tiles = (OH + BLOCK_OH - 1) / BLOCK_OH; int d_tile = spatial_block / (num_h_tiles * num_w_tiles); int rem = spatial_block % (num_h_tiles * num_w_tiles); int h_tile = rem / num_w_tiles; int w_tile = rem % num_w_tiles; int d0 = d_tile * BLOCK_OD; int h0 = h_tile * BLOCK_OH; int w0 = w_tile * BLOCK_OW; int tid = threadIdx.x; int w_out = tid % BLOCK_OW; int h_out = (tid / BLOCK_OW) % BLOCK_OH; int d_out = tid / (BLOCK_OW * BLOCK_OH); int d = d0 + d_out; int h = h0 + h_out; int w = w0 + w_out; bool valid = (d < OD && h < OH && w < OW); extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_weight = smem; float* smem_input = smem + NUM_WEIGHTS; float acc[BLOCK_OC]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < BLOCK_OC; i++) acc[i] = 0.0f; for (int ic_base = 0; ic_base < IC; ic_base += IC_BLOCK) { // Load weights for (int widx = tid; widx < NUM_WEIGHTS; widx += blockDim.x) { int kw = widx % 3; int wh = (widx / 3) % 3; int wd = (widx / 9) % 3; int ic_local = (widx / 27) % IC_BLOCK; int oc_local = widx / (27 * IC_BLOCK); int oc = oc_block + oc_local; int ic = ic_base + ic_local; if (oc < OC && ic < IC) { int widx_global = ((((oc * 3 + wd) * 3 + wh) * 3 + kw) * IC + ic); smem_weight[widx] = weight[widx_global]; } else { smem_weight[widx] = 0.0f; } } // Load input tile for (int iidx = tid; iidx < IC_BLOCK * IN_TILE_SIZE; iidx += blockDim.x) { int ic_local = iidx / IN_TILE_SIZE; int sp = iidx % IN_TILE_SIZE; int iw_sm = sp % IN_W; int ih_sm = (sp / IN_W) % IN_H; int id_sm = sp / (IN_W * IN_H); int ic = ic_base + ic_local; int iw_in = w0 - 2 + iw_sm; int ih_in = h0 - 2 + ih_sm; int id_in = d0 - 2 + id_sm; if (ic < IC && id_in >= 0 && id_in < ID && ih_in >= 0 && ih_in < IH && iw_in >= 0 && iw_in < IW) { int in_idx = (((batch * IC + ic) * ID + id_in) * IH + ih_in) * IW + iw_in; smem_input[iidx] = input[in_idx]; } else { smem_input[iidx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); if (valid) { #pragma unroll for (int ic_local = 0; ic_local < IC_BLOCK; ic_local++) { #pragma unroll for (int kd = 0; kd < 3; kd++) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int id_sm = d_out + kd; int ih_sm = h_out + kh; int iw_sm = w_out + kw; float in_val = smem_input[((ic_local * IN_D + id_sm) * IN_H + ih_sm) * IN_W + iw_sm]; #pragma unroll for (int oc_local = 0; oc_local < BLOCK_OC; oc_local++) { float w_val = smem_weight[((oc_local * IC_BLOCK + ic_local) * 9 + kd * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw]; acc[oc_local] += in_val * w_val; } } } } } } __syncthreads(); } float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sum_sq = 0.0f; if (valid) { #pragma unroll for (int oc_local = 0; oc_local < BLOCK_OC; oc_local++) { int oc = oc_block + oc_local; if (oc < OC) { float val = acc[oc_local]; val = fmaxf(val, 0.0f); int out_idx = (((batch * OC + oc) * OD + d) * OH + h) * OW + w; output[out_idx] = val; local_sum += val; local_sum_sq += val * val; } } } local_sum = block_reduce_sum(local_sum); local_sum_sq = block_reduce_sum(local_sum_sq); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { int block_linear = batch * gridDim.y * gridDim.x + blockIdx.y * gridDim.x + blockIdx.x; partial_sums[block_linear] = local_sum; partial_sum_sq[block_linear] = local_sum_sq; } } __global__ void reduce_stats_kernel( const float* __restrict__ partial_sums, const float* __restrict__ partial_sum_sq, float* __restrict__ group_mean, float* __restrict__ group_inv_std, int N, int num_groups, int blocks_per_group, int count, float eps ) { int batch = blockIdx.y; int g = blockIdx.x; int idx = batch * num_groups + g; float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sum_sq = 0.0f; int base = idx * blocks_per_group; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < blocks_per_group; i += blockDim.x) { local_sum += partial_sums[base + i]; local_sum_sq += partial_sum_sq[base + i]; } local_sum = block_reduce_sum(local_sum); local_sum_sq = block_reduce_sum(local_sum_sq); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { float mean = local_sum / count; float var = local_sum_sq / count - mean * mean; var = fmaxf(var, 0.0f); group_mean[idx] = mean; group_inv_std[idx] = rsqrtf(var + eps); } } __global__ void group_norm_apply_kernel( float* __restrict__ output, const float* __restrict__ group_mean, const float* __restrict__ group_inv_std, const float* __restrict__ gamma, const float* __restrict__ beta, int N, int C, int D, int H, int W, int num_groups, int channels_per_group ) { int n = blockIdx.y; int c = blockIdx.x; int g = c / channels_per_group; int spatial_size = D * H * W; int base_idx = ((n * C + c) * D * H * W); float mean = group_mean[n * num_groups + g]; float inv_std = group_inv_std[n * num_groups + g]; float g_val = gamma[c]; float b_val = beta[c]; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < spatial_size; idx += blockDim.x) { float x = output[base_idx + idx]; float y = (x - mean) * inv_std * g_val + b_val; output[base_idx + idx] = y; } } torch::Tensor forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor gamma, torch::Tensor beta, float eps ) { int N = input.size(0); int IC = input.size(1); int ID = input.size(2); int IH = input.size(3); int IW = input.size(4); int OC = weight.size(0); int OD = ID; // Because of padding=2 in conv3d equivalence, but wait... /// Actually, for Conv3d with P=2 on (32,32,32) with k=3, output = 32 + 4 - 3 + 1? No. // Output = (ID + 2*2 - 3) / 1 + 1 = ID + 2 = 34. int OH = IH + 2; int OW = IW + 2; auto output = torch::empty({N, OC, OD + 2, OH, OW}, input.options()); int num_groups = gamma.size(0) / 1; // Actually gamma is per-channel, shape (C,). // But num_groups is a parameter. Let me pass it in. } ``` Actually, I need to pass `num_groups` and `channels_per_group` explicitly, or compute them. Also, OD=ID+2, OH=IH+2, OW=IW+2. For the partial sums buffer: int spatial_tiles = ((OW + BLOCK_OW - 1) / BLOCK_OW) * ((OH + BLOCK_OH - 1) / BLOCK_OH) * ((OD + BLOCK_OD - 1) / BLOCK_OD); int oc_tiles = (OC + BLOCK_OC - 1) / BLOCK_OC; int total_blocks = N * oc_tiles * spatial_tiles; auto partial_sums = torch::empty({total_blocks}, input.options()); auto partial_sum_sq = torch::empty({total_blocks}, input.options()); For the mean and inv_std: auto group_mean = torch::empty({N, num_groups}, input.options()); auto group_inv_std = torch::empty({N, num_groups}, input.options()); Launch configs: conv kernel: dim3 grid(spatial_tiles, oc_tiles, N); dim3 block(BLOCK_OW * BLOCK_OH * BLOCK_OD); size_t smem_size = (BLOCK_OC * IC_BLOCK * 27 + IC_BLOCK * (BLOCK_OD + 2) * (BLOCK_OH + 2) * (BLOCK_OW + 2)) * sizeof(float); reduce_stats_kernel: dim3 grid(num_groups, N, 1); dim3 block(256); group_norm_apply_kernel: dim3 grid(OC, N, 1); // one block per channel dim3 block(256); Wait, for the group_norm_apply_kernel, if we launch OC*N blocks (2048), and N=16, OC=128, that's fine. reasoning
Good, it compiled. Let me now check correctness. Since ConvTranspose3d is mathematically equivalent to transposed convolution, which is equivalent to convolving with a flipped, permuted version, but I need to make sure I got this right. Let me think about the relationship... In PyTorch, ConvTranspose3d also pads internally. For a kernel_size=3, stride=1, the output is 2 larger than input in each spatial dimension, same as my OD/OH/OW computation. Let me run correctness first.

For `nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size)`: - weight shape: (in_channels, out_channels, *kernel_size) For `nn.Conv3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size)`: - weight shape: (out_channels, in_channels, *kernel_size) Yes, the first two dims are swapped. And I handle this correctly by permuting. OK, I think I should just try a different approach: use PyTorch for the conv, and write a custom kernel for ReLU+GroupNorm. That should definitely be correct and still provide a speedup. Actually, let me first try one more thing: implement the entire forward pass in a single kernel, but with a simpler algorithm. Maybe use im2col or gemm-like approach. Or, even better, let me just check whether the error is actually in my transform by doing explicit element-by-element comparison. Actually, I just realized something else. Let me re-read the prompt carefully. "Model that performs a transposed 3D convolution, applies ReLU, and then applies group normalization." And the constructor: `self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, bias=bias)` So by default, stride=1, padding=0, output_padding=0, dilation=1, groups=1. For kernel_size=3, this gives output size = input_size + 2 on each spatial dim. Now, `nn.ConvTranspose3d` in PyTorch computes: output_size = (input_size - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1 = (32 - 1) * 1 - 0 + 1 * 2 + 0 + 1 = 31 + 2 + 1 = 34. This matches what I expect. Now, I think the most likely explanation for the 0.003 difference is that PyTorch/cuDNN is using TF32, and my kernel isn't. Let me check if setting `torch.backends.cudnn.allow_tf32 = False` before running the reference would help... but I can't control the evaluation environment. Actually, wait. In PyTorch, even with TF32 enabled, the tolerance of 1e-4 should still hold for many cases. But 0.003 is well above 1e-4. Let me reconsider whether there might be a bug in my kernel after all. I've checked the math thoroughly, so maybe it's a runtime issue. One thing I just noticed: in my block_reduce_sum function, I declared `__shared__ float sdata[8];` inside the function. But this function is called twice in the same kernel: ```cpp local_sum = block_reduce_sum(local_sum); local_sum_sq = block_reduce_sum(local_sum_sq); ``` The second call reuses the same shared memory array `sdata[8]`, and there's a `__syncthreads()` between the two calls at the end of `block_reduce_sum`. But wait, looking at `block_reduce_sum`: ```cpp __inline__ __device__ float block_reduce_sum(float val) { __shared__ float sdata[8]; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / WARP_SIZE; int lane_id = threadIdx.x % WARP_SIZE; val = warp_reduce_sum(val); if (lane_id == 0) sdata[warp_id] = val; __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { val = (lane_id < 8) ? sdata[lane_id] : 0.0f; val = warp_reduce_sum(val); } __syncthreads(); // <-- This syncthreads is inside the function return val; } ``` The return statement is AFTER `__syncthreads()`. And then I call the function again. Between the two calls, ALL threads in the block have reached the `__syncthreads()` at the end of the first call, so it's safe. BUT, there's no `__syncthreads()` between: ```cpp local_sum = block_reduce_sum(local_sum); local_sum_sq = block_reduce_sum(local_sum_sq); ``` Wait, there IS a __syncthreads() at the end of each call, so after the first call, all threads are synchronized. Then the second call begins. This is fine. Hmm, but I realize the __syncthreads() at the end of the first call is redundant because the function is inline, and after returning, the code continues. But the threads ARE synchronized before the function returns, so it's correct. Wait, actually there's a subtle issue. The `__syncthreads()` inside `block_reduce_sum` is fine for the function itself, but what about the control flow divergence? ```cpp if (warp_id == 0) { val = (lane_id < 8) ? sdata[lane_id] : 0.0f; val = warp_reduce_sum(val); } __syncthreads(); ``` Threads in warp_id != 0 skip the if block but still hit the `__syncthreads()`. This is fine because `__syncthreads()` must be called by all threads in the block, and threads in warp_id != 0 do call it. OK this is not the issue. I'm going to try a completely different strategy: use PyTorch's `F.conv_transpose3d` in my custom kernel wrapper, then fuse the ReLU and GroupNorm in a custom CUDA kernel. This way, I know the conv part is exactly correct, and I just need to optimize the ReLU+GroupNorm part. Let me write this simpler version.

callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "import torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nimport torch.nn.functional as F\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\ncuda_source = \"\"\"\n#include reasoning
The issue is that I tried to use `c10::nullopt` in Python code. I need to handle this differently in Python language. Let me think about how to handle the optional bias in Python when calling a C++ function that takes `c10::optional<torch::Tensor>`. In PyTorch's Python bindings, `c10::optional<Tensor>` translates to accepting an optional tensor. From Python, we can pass `None` or a tensor. Let me write a simpler version that just uses PyTorch operations for the conv transpose, then fuses ReLU + group norm into a single kernel. This approach is simpler and less error-prone.

__syncthreads();\n local_sum_sq = block_reduce_sum(local_sum_sq, shared);\n \n float mean, inv_std;\n if (threadIdx.x == 0) {\n int count = channels_per_group * total_spatial;\n mean = local_sum / g_val + b_val;\n }\n }\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor forward(\n torch::Tensor input,\n torch::Tensor weight,\n torch::Tensor bias,\n bool has_bias,\n torch::Tensor gamma,\n torch::Tensor beta,\n int
